    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.2: Classification
    Lesson 1.2.1: r noninvasive measures), abnormal diastolic dysfunction.
    Lesson 1.2.2: Class I (Mild): Patients with cardiac disease but NO limitation of physical activity.
    Lesson 1.2.3: Class II (Mild): Patients with cardiac disease but slight limitation of physical activity.
    Lesson 1.2.4: Class III (Moderate): Patients with cardiac disease but marked limitation of physical activity.
  Mini Chapter 1.3: Epidemiology
    Lesson 1.3.1: The lifetime risk of HF at age 55 years is 33% for men and 28% for women.

  Mini Chapter 1.6: Clinical Picture
    Lesson 1.6.1: Congestive findings (1)
    Lesson 1.6.2: Exertional dyspnea: this may be a manifestation of
    Lesson 1.6.3: Bendopnea: shortness of breath when leaning forward. Seen in cases of advanced HF.
    Lesson 1.6.4: Increased jugular venous pulsation (JVP) ≥ 8 cmH2O
    Lesson 1.6.5: Crackles/pulmonary edema/pleural effusions.
  Mini Chapter 1.7: The most reliable congestive findings are
    Lesson 1.7.1: Low‐output findings: (also known as “cold” signs) correlate with a more advanced HF stage.
    Lesson 1.7.2: Severe fatigue, increased time to recover after exercise.
    Lesson 1.7.3: Cold, clammy extremities.
    Lesson 1.7.4: Valvar theory: sudden limitation of longitudinal expansion of LV wall during diastole
    Lesson 1.7.5: Compensatory tachycardia.
    Lesson 1.7.6: Abdominal pain may result from functional bowel ischemia or from liver distension.
  Mini Chapter 1.8: Investigations
    Lesson 1.8.1: Diagnostic tests in all patients with suspected HF
    Lesson 1.8.2: Q waves usually point towards ischemic cardiomyopathy.
    Lesson 1.8.3: Advanced AV block is frequently seen in myocarditis and infiltrative disease.
    Lesson 1.8.4: LBBB with QRS > 130 ms has therapeutic implications (benefit from CRT).
    Lesson 1.8.5: BNP takes 1 hour to rise and may be initially normal in hyperacute HF.
    Lesson 1.8.6: BNP is mostly cleared by tissue peptidase, but is partially cleared renally.
    Lesson 1.8.7: BNP tends to be higher in the elderly, in women, in AF, in renal failure, and in cirrhosis.
    Lesson 1.8.8: Heart failure

    Lesson 1.8.33: Echo assesses for other forms of HF
    Lesson 1.8.34: Isolated RV failure with its various causes (e.g., pulmonary hypertension, ASD).
    Lesson 1.8.35: Right sided HF mimickers: constrictive pericarditis, tamponade.
    Lesson 1.8.36: Specialized diagnostic tests for selected patients with chronic heart failure
    Lesson 1.8.37: Cardiac MRI has four major applications in HF
    Lesson 1.8.38: Diagnosis of myocarditis in unexplained cardiomyopathy or unexplained, large troponin elevation.
    Lesson 1.8.39: Viability assessment in ischemic cardiomyopathy.
    Lesson 1.8.40: therwise suitable for coronary revascularization.
    Lesson 1.8.41: Patients with a history of symptomatic ventricular arrhythmia or aborted cardiac arrest.
    Lesson 1.8.42: Stress echocardiography
    Lesson 1.8.43: In patients with unexplained dyspnoea and suspected HFpEF, stress echo help clarify the diagnosis.

  Mini Chapter 1.21: Three compensatory mechanisms occur in HF and are ultimately harmful
    Lesson 1.21.1: f acute and chronic heart failure.
    Lesson 1.21.2: Pharmacological Treatment
    Lesson 1.21.3: How to use?
    Lesson 1.21.4: Be cautious in cases of
    Lesson 1.21.5: If urea, creatinine, or K+ does rise excessively
    Lesson 1.21.6: Reverse the deleterious and apoptotic effects of catecholamines on the heart.
    Lesson 1.21.7: Reverse remodeling and reduce cardiac chamber size by reducing LV wall stress.
    Lesson 1.21.8: Prevent arrhythmias.
    Lesson 1.21.9: The three agents that have been shown to improve survival and outcomes in HF are
    Lesson 1.21.10: Keys to β‐blocker therapy
    Lesson 1.21.11: Start “low and slow”: Patients have to be euvolemic and stable.
    Lesson 1.21.12: The benefit in HF mainly results from
    Lesson 1.21.13: Blockade of the myocardial aldosterone receptors;
    Lesson 1.21.14: Increase in potassium (antiarrythmic effect);
    Lesson 1.21.15: Reduction of the tubular resistance to loop diuretics.
    Lesson 1.21.16: If gynecomastia develops, eplerenone may be used instead of spironolactone.
    Lesson 1.21.17: How to use
    Lesson 1.21.18: Start with a low dose and consider dose up-titration after 4-8 weeks.
    Lesson 1.21.19: Enhancing diuresis, natriuresis and myocardial relaxation and anti-remodelling.
    Lesson 1.21.20: Inhibiting renin and aldosterone secretion.
    Lesson 1.21.21: Start with a low dose.
    Lesson 1.21.22: Double the dose at not less than 2-week intervals in the community, monitoring tolerability.
    Lesson 1.21.23: Identify the risk factors predisposing to ketoacidosis and eliminate them if possible.
    Lesson 1.21.24: Monitor fluid balance regularly. Consider an adjustment of diuretic therapy and fluid intake.
    Lesson 1.21.25: nly be used with care.
    Lesson 1.21.26: Re-check (Urea, creatinine, K+) 1-2 weeks after an initiation and after any increase in dose.
    Lesson 1.21.27: Insufficient diuretic response/diuretic resistance
    Lesson 1.21.28: Check adherence and fluid/salt intake.
    Lesson 1.21.29: Increase a dose of diuretic.
    Lesson 1.21.30: Consider switching from furosemide to bumetanide or torasemide.
    Lesson 1.21.31: Add an MRA/increase dose of an MRA.
    Lesson 1.21.32: Combine loop diuretic and thiazide/metolazone.
    Lesson 1.21.33: Administer loop diuretic twice (or more times) daily or on empty stomach.
    Lesson 1.21.34: Consider short-term i.v. infusion of loop diuretic.
    Lesson 1.21.35: Consider ultrafiltration.
    Lesson 1.21.36: The combination may also be used instead of ACE‐I/ARB in case of intolerance to ACE‐I/ARB.
    Lesson 1.21.37: Digoxin
    Lesson 1.21.38: ptions cannot be pursued.
    Lesson 1.21.39: n the cumulative endpoint of CV death and hospitalization.
    Lesson 1.21.40: Cardiac myosin activator (Omecamtiv mecarbil)

  Mini Chapter 1.38: In HFrEF patients with sinus rhythm
    Lesson 1.38.1: CRT is recommended if QRS duration ≥ 150 ms, and LBBB QRS morphology.
    Lesson 1.38.2: CRT should be considered if QRS duration 130-149 ms, and LBBB QRS morphology.
    Lesson 1.38.3: CRT should be considered if QRS duration ≥ 150 ms, and non-LBBB QRS morphology
    Lesson 1.38.4: CRT may be considered if QRS duration 130-149 ms, and non-LBBB QRS morphology

    Lesson 1.40.1: Other therapeutic measures
    Lesson 1.40.2: Avoidance of harmful medications
    Lesson 1.40.3: Non‐dihydropyridines CCBs (negative inotropic effect) (1).
    Lesson 1.40.4: Diabetes medications: Thiazolidinediones (sodium retention).
    Lesson 1.40.5: Cilostazol (antiplatelet drug and a vasodilator; ventricular tachyarrhythmias).
    Lesson 1.40.6: Licorice (mineralocorticoid excess; sodium retention and hypokalemia).
  Mini Chapter 1.41: Prognosis
    Lesson 1.41.1: Predictors of HF hospitalizations: AF, higher BMI, higher HbA1c, and low eGFR.
    Lesson 1.41.2: Several factors negatively affect prognosis in HF patients
    Lesson 1.41.3: Severely decreased EF (< 30%).
    Lesson 1.41.4: Refractory functional class IIIB or IV (1‐year mortality 30-50%).
  Mini Chapter 1.42: Prevention of heart failure
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.43: In order to prevent or delay the onset of HF
    Lesson 1.43.1: Treatment of hypertension is recommended.
    Lesson 1.43.2: Treatment with statins is recommended in patients at high risk of CV disease.
    Lesson 1.43.3: SGLT2 inhibitors (canagliflozin, dapagliflozin, empagliflozin, ertugliflozin, sotagliflozin) are
    Lesson 1.43.4: Counselling against sedentary habit, obesity, cigarette smoking, and alcohol abuse is
  Mini Chapter 1.44: Follow-up of chronic heart failure

  Mini Chapter 1.45: Co-morbidities
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 1.46: Importance of co-morbidities in patients with heart failure
    Lesson 1.46.1: Aggravate HF symptoms and further impair quality of life.
    Lesson 1.46.2: Contribute to the burden of hospitalizations and mortality.
    Lesson 1.46.3: f interventions is therefore often lacking in the presence of co-morbidities.
    Lesson 1.46.4: Drugs used to treat co-morbidities may worsen HF (e.g. NSAIDs given for arthritis).
    Lesson 1.46.5: Interaction between drugs used to treat HF and those used to treat co-morbidities.
    Lesson 1.46.6: Chronic coronary syndrome
    Lesson 1.46.7: Diltiazem and verapamil increase HF-related events in patients with HFrEF and are contraindicated.
    Lesson 1.46.8: Atrial Fibrillation
    Lesson 1.46.9: Potential causes or precipitating factors (e.g, hyperthyroidism, MV disease) should be corrected.
    Lesson 1.46.10: Valvular heart disease: (For more details, See chapter 12)
    Lesson 1.46.11: Hypertension
    Lesson 1.46.12: Treatment of HFrEF is similar in hypertensive and normotensive patients.

    Lesson 1.46.14: Stroke
    Lesson 1.46.15: Diabetes

    Lesson 1.46.19: Anaemia and Iron deficiency
    Lesson 1.46.20: Iron deficiency impairs myocardial and musculoskeletal metabolism, even in the absence of anemia.
    Lesson 1.46.21: Kidney dysfunction
    Lesson 1.46.22: f nonarrhythmic causes of death.
    Lesson 1.46.23: Electrolyte disorders
    Lesson 1.46.24: f poor outcomes in patients with acute or chronic HF.
    Lesson 1.46.25: In patients with K+ levels of 4.5-5.0 mEq/L
    Lesson 1.46.26: RAAS inhibitor therapy can be initiated/uptitrated.
    Lesson 1.46.27: If K+ levels rise above 5.0 mEq/L, initiate an approved K+-lowering agent.
    Lesson 1.46.28: In patients with K+ levels of 5.0-6.5 mEq/L
    Lesson 1.46.29: An approved K+-lowering agent should be initiated.
    Lesson 1.46.30: If levels < 5.0 mEq/L are detected, uptitrate RAAS inhibitor.
    Lesson 1.46.31: In patients with K+ levels of > 6.5 mEq/L
    Lesson 1.46.32: It is recommended to discontinue/reduce RAAS inhibitor.

    Lesson 1.51.24: Device therapy e.g LVAD and CRT.

Chapter 2: Acute heart failure
  Mini Chapter 2.1: Acute HF presentation encompasses three syndromes
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 2.2: Triggers of acute decompensation
    Lesson 2.2.1: Non‐compliance with medical therapy, salt and fluid restriction.
    Lesson 2.2.2: r the acceleration of AF can be secondary to HF rather than the cause of HF.
    Lesson 2.2.3: Increased sympathetic drive, stress-related cardiomyopathy.
    Lesson 2.2.4: Drug-induced (alcohol, NSAIDs, steroids, recreational drugs, and cardiotoxic chemotherapeutics).
  Mini Chapter 2.3: In systolic or diastolic dysfunction, three untoward effects occur
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 2.4: Notes
    Lesson 2.4.1: 40-50% of acute heart failure episodes have no known precipitant.
  Mini Chapter 2.5: Classification
    Lesson 2.5.1: Increased afterload

  Mini Chapter 2.6: Clinical picture/Profiles
    Lesson 2.6.1: Orthopnea (90% sensitive), crackles (uncommon), peripheral edema, pleural effusions, ascites.
    Lesson 2.6.2: Elevated JVP, S3, loud P2 that is heard at the lower left sternum or apex (usually with split S2).
    Lesson 2.6.3: Cool and/or cyanotic extremities.
    Lesson 2.6.4: Severe fatigue, drowsiness, Cheyne-Stokes respiration.
    Lesson 2.6.5: Severe worsening of renal failure.
    Lesson 2.6.6: Hyponatremia.
    Lesson 2.6.7: Poor or no response to diuretics.
    Lesson 2.6.8: Class I: No clinical signs of HF.
    Lesson 2.6.9: Class II: HF with rales and S3 gallop.
    Lesson 2.6.10: Class III: Frank acute pulmonary oedema.

  Mini Chapter 2.11: Treatment
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 2.12: Management of AHF consists of
    Lesson 2.12.1: Urgent Care
    Lesson 2.12.2: ↓ Venous return → ↓ RV preload → ↓ LV preload.
    Lesson 2.12.3: ↑ Pulmonary vascular resistance → ↑ RV afterload → RV enlargement → ↓ LV Compliance.
    Lesson 2.12.4: Hospitalization
    Lesson 2.12.5: Criteria for ICU/CCU admission: include any of the following
    Lesson 2.12.6: Need for intubation (or already intubated).
    Lesson 2.12.7: Signs/symptoms of hypoperfusion.
    Lesson 2.12.8: Oxygen saturation (SaO2) < 90% (despite supplemental oxygen).
    Lesson 2.12.9: Use of accessory muscles for breathing, respiratory rate > 25/min.
    Lesson 2.12.10: Heart rate < 40 or > 130 bpm, SBP < 90 mmHg.
    Lesson 2.12.11: Diagnosis and treatment of triggers
    Lesson 2.12.12: Tachyarrhythmias or severe bradycardia/conduction disturbance
  Mini Chapter 2.13: Oxygen and ventilatory support
    (treated as Lesson itself)

  Mini Chapter 2.19: Prognosis
    Lesson 2.19.1: There are four major prognostic risk factors
    Lesson 2.19.2: ↑ BUN or creatinine on admission.
    Lesson 2.19.3: SBP < 115 mmHg.
    Lesson 2.19.4: “Cold” low‐output HF.
    Lesson 2.19.5: Positive troponin (especially > 1 ng/ml, regardless of the presence of ischemia).
    Lesson 2.19.6: The mortality and rehospitalization rates at 60 and 90 days are 8-10% and 30%, respectively.
    Lesson 2.19.7: The mortality and rehospitalization rates at 1 year are 30% and 50%, respectively.
  Mini Chapter 2.20: Notes about pharmacological treatment used in treatment of AHF
    Lesson 2.20.1: Furosemide dosing: Loop diuretics are the mainstay of acute HF therapy.
    Lesson 2.20.2: Laboratory monitoring is necessary to detect adverse metabolic effects
    Lesson 2.20.3: Hypokalemia, Hypomagnesemia, Hypocalcemia.
    Lesson 2.20.4: Diuretic resistance
    Lesson 2.20.5: Several mechanisms are implicated
    Lesson 2.20.6: Acute, intrinsic kidney injury unresponsive to the diuretic (e.g., ATN).
    Lesson 2.20.7: Activation of the renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system.
    Lesson 2.20.8: Post‐diuretic rebound effect, i.e., tubular reabsorption of sodium in between doses.
    Lesson 2.20.9: Management
    Lesson 2.20.10: If there is a moderate response to the maximal single dose of furosemide
    Lesson 2.20.11: r to a necessary drop of the preload in patients with acute or diastolic HF.
    Lesson 2.20.12: Vasodilators should be used with caution in patients with significant mitral or aortic stenosis.
    Lesson 2.20.13: Intravenous nitroglycerin (NTG)
    Lesson 2.20.14: r ACE‐I.
    Lesson 2.20.15: Intravenous nitroprusside
    Lesson 2.20.16: f LV systolic dysfunction (1).
    Lesson 2.20.17: Nesiritide
    Lesson 2.20.18: Serelaxin (investigational)
    Lesson 2.20.19: Dobutamine
    Lesson 2.20.20: Mechanism of action: Dobutamine has marked β1‐agonist, and less marked β2‐ and α1‐agonist effect.
    Lesson 2.20.21: Milrinone
    Lesson 2.20.22: Dose: Bolus: 25-75 µg/kg over 10–20 min .. Maintenance: 0.375-0.75 µg/kg/min.
    Lesson 2.20.23: utput to catch the vasodilatory effect, therefore preventing hypotension.
    Lesson 2.20.24: Levosimendan
    Lesson 2.20.25: Mechanism of action
    Lesson 2.20.26: Open of ATP-sensitive potassium channels → vasodilation.
    Lesson 2.20.27: Dopamine
    Lesson 2.20.28: Mechanism of action: Endogenous catecholamine, acting on both dopaminergic and adrenergic neurons.
    Lesson 2.20.29: Low dose (3-5 µg/kg/min.) stimulates dopaminergic receptors → renal and mesenteric vasodilation.
    Lesson 2.20.30: Large dose (> 10 µg/kg/min) stimulates alpha-adrenergic receptors.
    Lesson 2.20.31: Norepinephrine
    Lesson 2.20.32: Mechanism of action: have inotropic and vasoconstrictive effects.
    Lesson 2.20.33: Stimulates α1 and α2 receptors → Vasoconstriction → increases peripheral vascular resistance.
    Lesson 2.20.34: Acts on beta-1 adrenergic receptors → ↑ heart rate and cardiac output.
    Lesson 2.20.35: Dose: 0.2-1.0 µg/kg/min
    Lesson 2.20.36: Epinephrine (adrenaline)
    Lesson 2.20.37: Dose: 0.05-0.5 µg/kg/min
  Mini Chapter 2.21: RA pressure, pulmonary capillary pressure, and cardiac output, if

    Lesson 2.21.14: Causes of right sided HF
    Lesson 2.21.15: Acute pulmonary hypertension e.g pulmonary embolism, acute lung injury or ARDS.

    Lesson 2.22.2: Diagnosis of the underlying mechanism of RV failure

  Mini Chapter 3.6: Contraindications
    Lesson 3.6.1: Moderate or severe AI.
    Lesson 3.6.2: Abdominal aortic aneurysm.
    Lesson 3.6.3: Aortic stents or grafts.
    Lesson 3.6.4: HOCM (since intracavitary obstruction increases with afterload reduction).
    Lesson 3.6.5: Insertion complications (pseudo aneurysm formation, bleeding, vascular injury).
    Lesson 3.6.6: Aortic damage.
    Lesson 3.6.7: Systemic embolization.
    Lesson 3.6.8: Limb ischaemia.
    Lesson 3.6.9: Compartment syndrome.
    Lesson 3.6.10: Haematological effects (haemolysis and thrombocytopenia).
    Lesson 3.6.11: Malpositioning causing arterial occlusion
    Lesson 3.6.12: Too low → renal and mesenteric ischaemia.
    Lesson 3.6.13: Timing errors worsening myocardial oxygen supply- demand ratio.
    Lesson 3.6.14: Balloon rupture (if blood enters balloon lumen may clot and cause difficulty in removing).
    Lesson 3.6.15: Infection
    Lesson 3.6.16: Daily chest X‐rays are performed to check the catheter position.

  Mini Chapter 3.22: There are several pump designs
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 3.23: Continuous-flow LVADs have a number of major advantages over previous pulsatile technology
    Lesson 3.23.1: Continuous-flow pumps eliminate the need for a blood pumping chamber and volume compensation.
    Lesson 3.23.2: A lighter, smaller pump is better suited for patients with a smaller body size.
    Lesson 3.23.3: Simple designs include only 1 internal moving part, the rotor, and no internal valves.
    Lesson 3.23.4: They are silent in operation.
    Lesson 3.23.5: The benefits of the smaller percutaneous lead: reduced infection risk and greater patient comfort.

    Lesson 4.52.1: In infants with DCM, we should consider
    Lesson 4.52.2: reversible causes (i.e. hypocalcaemic vitamin D-dependent rickets) and
    Lesson 4.52.3: CHD (aortic coarctation and ALCAPA, requiring immediate surgical management).
    Lesson 4.52.4: Isolated RCM is rare in infants, but a mixed RCM/HCM phenotype is more frequently encountered.
    Lesson 4.52.5: In infants with HCM, we should consider
    Lesson 4.52.6: Reversible causes (e.g., maternal diabetes, twin–twin syndrome, corticosteroid use).
    Lesson 4.52.7: xidation defects, and mitochondrial disorders.

  Mini Chapter 4.75: Prognosis
    Lesson 4.75.1: The worst prognosis is seen with the following three cardiomyopathies: (HAD)
    Lesson 4.75.2: Some DCMs may be reversible (1)
    Lesson 4.75.3: DCM associated with inflammatory/immune response: Myocarditis, Peripartum cardiomyopathy.
    Lesson 4.75.4: Besides GDMT, several additional predictors of reverse LV remodelling have been identified
    Lesson 4.75.5: Female sex, a non-ischaemic HF aetiology and the absence of digoxin use (IMPROVE-HF study).
    Lesson 4.75.6: Higher baseline LVEF.
    Lesson 4.75.7: Lower LV end-diastolic diameter.
    Lesson 4.75.8: Lower extent of LGE on CMR (indicative of lower interstitial replacement fibrosis).
    Lesson 4.75.9: TTN mutation (Higher rate of LV reverse remodeling in up to 70%).
    Lesson 4.75.10: Poor prognostic factors
    Lesson 4.75.11: Clinical: Male sex, advanced age (> 60 years), Black race, NYHA class (III-IV).
    Lesson 4.75.12: ECG: Presence of LBBB.
    Lesson 4.75.13: Biomarkers: Higher natriuretic peptide levels.
    Lesson 4.75.14: Biopsy-proven, Virus-negative post-myocarditis DCM.
    Lesson 4.75.15: Acute giant-cell and eosinophilic myocarditis
    Lesson 4.75.16: Cardiac sarcoidosis.

    Lesson 4.78.1: The following parameters suggest Arrhythmia-induced (vs Arrhythmia-aggravated) cardiomyopathy

    Lesson 4.78.5: LVEF improvement/normalization (reverse remodelling) following suppression of the arrhythmia.

    Lesson 4.97.7: Indication

    Lesson 4.99.4: Arrhythmic syncope in patients with definitive ARVC.

    Lesson 4.108.6: The thickening frequently involves both ventricles, not just the LV.

    Lesson 4.118.4: Chronotropic incompetence

    Lesson 4.118.7: Reduced global longitudinal strain

    Lesson 4.118.10: High focal/global T2

    Lesson 4.118.12: Elevated NT-proBNP
    Lesson 4.118.13: f the disease, before irreversible tissue fibrosis takes place.
    Lesson 4.118.14: r twice weekly to reduce serum ferritin to < 1000 ng/mL (or < 1000 𝜇g/L).
    Lesson 4.118.15: r malignancy.
    Lesson 4.118.16: nset of HF.

    Lesson 4.118.24: Management
    Lesson 4.118.25: Pulmonary valvuloplasty may be considered in children and infants with severe RVOT obstruction.
    Lesson 4.118.26: Surgical myectomy and orthotopic heart transplantation may be considered.
    Lesson 4.118.27: There is no relationship between the extent of neurological involvement and cardiac phenotype.
    Lesson 4.118.28: Mitochondrial iron storage is the pathologic hallmark of the disease.
    Lesson 4.118.29: Short PR interval and increased ECG voltages may represent useful diagnostic clues for GSDs.

  Mini Chapter 4.141: Features of HCM
    Lesson 4.141.1: Asymmetry
    Lesson 4.141.2: Pattern of LVH in HCM
    Lesson 4.141.3: Sigmoid HCM (40-50%): septal protuberance basal concave septum
    Lesson 4.141.4: Reverse Curve HCM (30-40%): convex septum, concentric LV cavity.
    Lesson 4.141.5: Apical HCM (10%): apical hypertrophy “Ace of spades”.
    Lesson 4.141.6: Neutral HCM (10%): straight septum.
    Lesson 4.141.7: LVOT obstruction
    Lesson 4.141.8: The marked septal hypertrophy may also contribute to RVOT obstruction, particularly in children.
    Lesson 4.141.9: Causes of MR in HOCM
    Lesson 4.141.10: Mitral valve abnormalities aggravate SAM and LVOT obstruction
    Lesson 4.141.11: Extreme elongation and prolapse of the posterior mitral leaflet (~9% of operated HOCM).
    Lesson 4.141.12: Papillary muscle insertion directly onto the anterior leaflet.
    Lesson 4.141.13: Chordal rupture.
  Mini Chapter 4.142: Less common forms of HCM

    Lesson 4.160.3: Differential diagnosis of LVOT obstruction
  Mini Chapter 4.161: Clinical features favouring hypertension only
    Lesson 4.161.1: Normal 12 lead ECG or isolated increased voltage without repolarisation abnormality.
    Lesson 4.161.2: Regression of LVH over 6-12 months after tight systolic blood pressure control (< 130 mmHg).
  Mini Chapter 4.162: Clinical features favouring hypertrophic cardiomyopathy
    Lesson 4.162.1: Family history of HCM.
    Lesson 4.162.2: RV hypertrophy.
    Lesson 4.162.3: LGE at the RV insertion points or localized to segments of maximum LV thickening on CMR.
    Lesson 4.162.4: Maximum LV wall thickness ≥ 15 mm (Caucasian); ≥ 20 mm (black).
    Lesson 4.162.5: Severe diastolic dysfunction.
    Lesson 4.162.6: Marked repolarisation abnormalities, conduction disease or Q-waves on 12 lead ECG.
  Mini Chapter 4.163: Treatment
    Lesson 4.163.1: General measures
    Lesson 4.163.2: Pharmacological treatment
    Lesson 4.163.3: f angina.
    Lesson 4.163.4: f symptoms and LVOT gradient (EXPLORER-HCM trial).
    Lesson 4.163.5: Acute therapy of acute HF or shock
    Lesson 4.163.6: bstruction and thus, paradoxically, worsen pulmonary edema.

    Lesson 4.167.1: Complications
    Lesson 4.167.2: AV block: 2% in surgical myectomy and 7–20% in alcoholic septal ablation.
    Lesson 4.167.3: Bundle branch block (LBBB in septal myectomy and RBBB in alcohol septal ablation),
    Lesson 4.167.4: Residual LVOT gradients (more in alcoholic septal ablation).
    Lesson 4.167.5: moderate-to-severe SAM-related mitral regurgitation; or
    Lesson 4.167.6: AF; or
    Lesson 4.167.7: moderate-to-severe left atrial dilatation.

  Mini Chapter 4.173: Established Clinical Risk Factors for HCM-SCD risk score
    Lesson 4.173.1: There is an increased risk of SCD in younger patients.
    Lesson 4.173.2: Sudden cardiac death is very rare below the age of 6 years.
    Lesson 4.173.3: Family history of SCD does not appear to be an independent risk factor for SCD in childhood HCM.
    Lesson 4.173.4: There is no evidence that the frequency, duration, or rate of NSVT influences the risk of SCD.
    Lesson 4.173.5: NSVT occurring during or immediately following exercise is very rare, but may be associated with a
    Lesson 4.173.6: bstruction
    Lesson 4.173.7: 2020 AHA/ACC HCM guidelines consider it as a major independent SCD risk factor. However, ESC
    Lesson 4.173.8: n CMR; LVEF < 50%. Source: 2023 ESC Guidelines for the management of cardiomyopathies.

  Mini Chapter 5.1: Main features of CCS
    Lesson 5.1.1: Natural history: Stable symptomatic or asymptomatic phases which may be interrupted by ACS.
    Lesson 5.1.2: ver time.

    Lesson 5.2.1: From 5-7% in women aged 45-64 years to 10-12% in women aged 65-84 and
    Lesson 5.2.2: From 4-7% in men aged 45-64 years to 12-14% in men aged 65-84.
  Mini Chapter 5.3: Prognosis
    Lesson 5.3.1: lder age, significant depression and more severe angina.
  Mini Chapter 5.4: Angina caused by fixed coronary obstruction

  Mini Chapter 5.18: Treatment
    Lesson 5.18.1: Event prevention: Anitplatelet drugs - Lipid lowering agents - RAAS blockers.
    Lesson 5.18.2: Smoking
    Lesson 5.18.3: Diet
    Lesson 5.18.4: Increase consumption of fruits and vegetables (≥ 200 g each per day).
    Lesson 5.18.5: 35-45 g of fibre per day, preferably from wholegrains.
    Lesson 5.18.6: Moderate consumption of nuts (30 g per day, unsalted).
    Lesson 5.18.7: 12 servings of fish per week (one to be oily fish).
    Lesson 5.18.8: Limited lean meat, low-fat dairy products, and liquid vegetable oils.
    Lesson 5.18.9: Saturated fats to account for < 10% of total energy intake; replace with polyunsaturated fats.
    Lesson 5.18.10: ≤ 5-6 g of salt per day.
    Lesson 5.18.11: If alcohol is consumed, limiting intake to ≤ 100 g/week or < 15 g/day is recommended.
    Lesson 5.18.12: Avoid energy-dense foods such as sugar-sweetened soft drinks.

    Lesson 7.2.1: A 12-lead ECG should be obtained as soon as possible at the point of FMC
    Lesson 7.2.2: Typically, ST-elevation measured at the J point, should be found in two contiguous leads and be
    Lesson 7.2.3: ≥ 0.25 mV in men < 40 years, ≥ 0.2 mV in men > 40 years, or ≥ 0.15 mV in women in leads V2-V3.
    Lesson 7.2.4: ≥ 0.1 mV in other leads (in the absence of LVH or LBBB).
    Lesson 7.2.5: Isolated posterior MI
    Lesson 7.2.6: Left main coronary obstruction or multivessel disease
    Lesson 7.2.7: cclusion, well before necrosis. The absence of wall-motion abnormalities excludes major MI.

    Lesson 7.42.1: Differential diagnosis
    Lesson 7.42.2: Arrhythmias (inappropriate bradycardia, advanced AV block, VT).
    Lesson 7.42.3: Treatment of RV shock (beside emergent reperfusion)
    Lesson 7.42.4: n TTE.
    Lesson 7.42.5: Hypoxemia should be aggressively treated as hypoxemia increases PVR and RV afterload.
    Lesson 7.42.6: Inhaled NO has shown to reduce RA pressure and PVR, and increase stroke volume, in RV MI.
    Lesson 7.42.7: Severe mitral regurgitation (MR)
    Lesson 7.42.8: Mechanism
    Lesson 7.42.9: Posterior leaflet tethering – A degree of ischemic MR is seen in ~30% of acute MI.
    Lesson 7.42.10: Treatment
    Lesson 7.42.11: Ventricular septal rupture (VSR): [~1% of MIs (only 0.2% of reperfused MIs)].
    Lesson 7.42.12: Free wall rupture
    Lesson 7.42.13: Dynamic LVOT obstruction: [1–2% of MIs and up to 12% of anterior MIs in women].
    Lesson 7.42.14: Escalate β‐blockers, NTG, and readminister anticoagulants.
    Lesson 7.42.15: ccur.
    Lesson 7.42.16: VF and sudden death: There are three types of VF (mostly characterized in fibrinolytic trials)
    Lesson 7.42.17: Sustained VT: There are two types of VT
    Lesson 7.42.18: Non‐sustained VT (NSVT)
    Lesson 7.42.19: Early NSVT (< 48 hours) is not associated with any impairment of short‐ or long‐term survival.
    Lesson 7.42.20: Late NSVT is associated with impaired long‐term survival.
    Lesson 7.42.21: Accelerated idioventricular rhythm = slow wide ventricular rhythm at a rate of 60–100 (120) bpm.
  Mini Chapter 7.43: Treatment
    Lesson 7.43.1: The sinus nodal artery originates from the proximal RCA (60%) or the LCx (40%).
    Lesson 7.43.2: The right bundle mainly has a single arterial supply from the LAD (first septal branch).
    Lesson 7.43.3: The left anterior fascicle has a single arterial supply from the LAD (first septal branch).
    Lesson 7.43.4: Dyskinesis without an aneurysm is much more common than a true aneurysm.
    Lesson 7.43.5: An aneurysm leads to increased preload and afterload, and a double mortality for the same EF.
    Lesson 7.43.6: On imaging, the distinction between a true LV aneurysm and a pseudoaneurysm is
    Lesson 7.43.7: MRI may be used in equivocal cases and shows loss of epicardial fat across the pseudoaneurysm.
    Lesson 7.43.8: Left
    Lesson 7.43.9: Treatment
    Lesson 7.43.10: ccurred within 3-4 months of MI.

Chapter 8: Non ST elevation ACS
  Mini Chapter 8.1: Definition
    Lesson 8.1.1: Rest angina.
    Lesson 8.1.2: Angina occurring within 2 weeks after a myocardial infarction (post‐infarction angina).
  Mini Chapter 8.2: Pathophysiology
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 8.3: The pathogenesis of NSTE-ACS involves four processes operating singly or in various combinations
    Lesson 8.3.1: f platelet GP IIb/IIIa.
    Lesson 8.3.2: Cardiac causes of secondary NSTE-ACS include: severe hypertension, acute HF, tachyarrhythmias.
    Lesson 8.3.3: Non‐cardiac causes of secondary NSTE-ACS include: GI bleed, severe anemia, hypoxia, sepsis.
  Mini Chapter 8.4: Diagnosis
    Lesson 8.4.1: Clinical presentation and ECG
    Lesson 8.4.2: Patients with acute chest pain and persistent (> 20 min) ST-elevation. This is termed (STEMI).
    Lesson 8.4.3: r the ECG may be normal (1).
    Lesson 8.4.4: Biomarkers
  Mini Chapter 8.5: Compared with standard cardiac troponin assays, hs-cTn assays
    Lesson 8.5.1: Have higher NPV for AMI.
    Lesson 8.5.2: Reduce the ‘troponin-blind’ interval leading to earlier detection of AMI.
    Lesson 8.5.3: f unstable angina.
    Lesson 8.5.4: Are associated with a 2-fold increase in the detection of type 2 MI.
    Lesson 8.5.5: Elevations beyond 5-fold the upper reference limit have high (> 90%) PPV for acute type 1 MI.
    Lesson 8.5.6: Elevations up to 3-fold the upper reference limit have only limited (50-60%) PPV for AMI.
    Lesson 8.5.7: It is common to detect circulating levels of cardiac troponin in healthy individuals.
    Lesson 8.5.8: Rapid ‘rule-in’ and ‘rule-out’ algorithms
    Lesson 8.5.9: Non-invasive imaging
    Lesson 8.5.10: Functional evaluation
    Lesson 8.5.11: Stress echocardiography has demonstrated superior prognostic value over exercise ECG.
    Lesson 8.5.12: SPECT is useful for the risk stratification of patients with acute chest pain suggestive of ACS.
    Lesson 8.5.13: Anatomical evaluation
    Lesson 8.5.14: CCTA allows visualization of the coronary arteries, and a normal scan excludes CAD.

  Mini Chapter 11.21: Mechanisms of mitral regurgitation
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 11.22: There are four major mechanisms of mitral regurgitation (Carpentier’s classification)
    Lesson 11.22.1: Type I: Normal leaflet motion.
    Lesson 11.22.2: Type II: Excessive leaflet motion
    Lesson 11.22.3: Type IIIa: Restricted leaflet motion during diastole and systole (i.e restricted opening)
    Lesson 11.22.4: Type IIIb: Restricted leaflet motion during systole (i.e restricted closure)
  Mini Chapter 11.23: Three additional mechanisms contribute to MR
    Lesson 11.23.1: Annular dilatation, which reduces leaflet coaptation.
  Mini Chapter 11.24: Causes of mitral regurgitation
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 11.25: Causes of acute MR
    Lesson 11.25.1: Papillary muscle rupture or acute functional MR occurring in the context of an acute MI.
    Lesson 11.25.2: Chordae tendinae rupture. Causes: idiopathic, mitral valve prolapse, endocarditis, trauma.
    Lesson 11.25.3: Acute functional MR secondary to an acute ventricular process (MI, myocarditis).
    Lesson 11.25.4: Primary MR: [Primary lesion of one or more components of the mitral valve apparatus]
    Lesson 11.25.5: Mitral Valve Prolapse (MVP)
    Lesson 11.25.6: There are two forms of MVP
    Lesson 11.25.7: MVP occurs in 1-2% of the population with a familial trend. Female‐to‐male ratio= 2:1.
    Lesson 11.25.8: MVP may be associated with tricuspid prolapse (in ~40%) and aortic valve prolapse (in ~10%).
    Lesson 11.25.9: 15% of MVP cases progress to severe MR at a mean follow‐up duration of 15 years.
    Lesson 11.25.10: Complications in serious MVP subgroup: (1) severe MR; (2) endocarditis; (3) VT/sudden death.
    Lesson 11.25.11: Secondary (Functional) MR
    Lesson 11.25.12: r in patients with HFpEF.
    Lesson 11.25.13: Other causes of MR
    Lesson 11.25.14: Systolic anterior motion of the anterior leaflet and chordae in HOCM.
    Lesson 11.25.15: Causes of transient severe MR
    Lesson 11.25.16: f annular contraction reduces the coaptation of the tented leaflet and contributes to MR.

    Lesson 11.36.1: Age‐related calcific degeneration (most common cause)
    Lesson 11.36.2: Bicuspid aortic valve (BAV)
    Lesson 11.36.3: BAV is the most common congenital heart disease (~1.3% of the population).
    Lesson 11.36.4: Patients with BAV have a high risk of ascending aortic dilatation or dissection.
    Lesson 11.36.5: Patients with BAV should undergo, at least once, a screening with CT to assess the whole aorta.

  Mini Chapter 11.40: Other features suggestive of severe AS
    Lesson 11.40.1: Dimensionless index
    Lesson 11.40.2: Peak LVOT velocity is < 1 m/s.
    Lesson 11.40.3: LVOT velocity/aortic valve velocity is ≤ 0.25.
  Mini Chapter 11.41: Pitfalls
    Lesson 11.41.1: Like any velocity assessment, it is angle‐dependent.
  Mini Chapter 11.42: Additional diagnostic and prognostic parameters
    Lesson 11.42.1: f valve and vascular calcification, and feasibility of vascular access.
    Lesson 11.42.2: Catheterization
  Mini Chapter 11.43: Categories of Aortic Stenosis
    Lesson 11.43.1: Low‐gradient AS with AVA ≤ 1 cm2 and low EF < 50%
    Lesson 11.43.2: In case of mean gradient < 30 mmHg, AVA ≤ 1cm2, and EF < 50% (esp. < 40%), the patient may have
    Lesson 11.43.3: Truly severe AS with a low gradient resulting from the low cardiac output, or
    Lesson 11.43.4: Low‐gradient AS with AVA ≤ 1 cm2 but Normal EF (Paradoxical low‐flow/Low‐gradient Severe AS)
    Lesson 11.43.5: Severe preload reduction (e.g., hypovolemia)
    Lesson 11.43.6: Associated mitral valve disease that reduces the flow across the aortic valve.
    Lesson 11.43.7: 3rd step: Measure the stroke volume index and valvuloarterial impedance.
    Lesson 11.43.8: Typical symptoms without explanation.
    Lesson 11.43.9: Elderly patient (> 70 years).
    Lesson 11.43.10: LVH (additional history of Hypertension to be considered).
    Lesson 11.43.11: Reduced LV longitudinal function without other explanation.
    Lesson 11.43.12: Mean gradient 30-40 mmHg (1)
    Lesson 11.43.13: AVA ≤ 0.8 cm2
    Lesson 11.43.14: Low flow (SVi < 35 ml/m2) confirmed by techniques other than standard
    Lesson 11.43.15: Calcium Score by MSCT
    Lesson 11.43.16: Severe AS very likely: Men ≥ 3000, Women ≥ 1600
    Lesson 11.43.17: Severe AS likely: Men ≥ 2000, Women ≥ 1200
    Lesson 11.43.18: Severe AS unlikely: Men <1600, Women < 800
  Mini Chapter 11.44: Notes
    Lesson 11.44.1: Diagnosis of subvalvular or supravalvular AS
    Lesson 11.44.2: Subvalvular AS
    Lesson 11.44.3: It is commonly associated with VSD.
    Lesson 11.44.4: Supravalvular AS
    Lesson 11.44.5: Aortic valve thickening and regurgitation may develop.
    Lesson 11.44.6: This condition is frequently associated with peripheral pulmonary artery stenosis.
    Lesson 11.44.7: Surgical therapy is indicated if: Symptomatic, LVH, or Asymptomatic with mean gradient > 50 mmHg.

    Lesson 11.105.1: Bioprosthetic valve degeneration depends on
    Lesson 11.105.2: PV thrombosis is a complex multifactorial phenomenon, occurs due to
    Lesson 11.105.3: Hemodynamic Factors: e.g., Low cardiac output, prosthetic hemodynamic profile, or hyperviscosity.
    Lesson 11.105.4: Hemostatic Factors: e.g., hypercoagulable state, significant tissue injury.
    Lesson 11.105.5: It presents with HF, hemodynamic compromise and loss of the prosthetic click on exam.
    Lesson 11.105.6: Causes: (all these processes may also be seen with a bioprosthetic valve, at a lower frequency)
    Lesson 11.105.7: Thrombosis (more common than pannus formation).
    Lesson 11.105.8: Early postoperative prosthetic obstruction may be due to severe annular calcification.
    Lesson 11.105.9: A paraprosthetic leak is observed with both mechanical and bioprosthetic valves.
    Lesson 11.105.10: EOAPr= CSALVOT x VTILVOT / VTIPr
    Lesson 11.105.11: They often do well in the intermediate term but have an impaired long‐term survival and prognosis.
    Lesson 11.105.12: PPM is severe or associated with moderate or severe valve stenosis.
    Lesson 11.105.13: Mean transprosthetic gradient is high (30-35 mmHg).
    Lesson 11.105.14: If patient develops heart failure symptoms or LV systolic dysfunction.
    Lesson 11.105.15: CABG: ~1–5% - Redo CABG: ~10%
    Lesson 11.105.16: MVR: ~6%
    Lesson 11.105.17: MVR in patients > 70 years: 14% - MVR + CABG: 11% (2) - MV repair: ~1–3%
    Lesson 11.105.18: AVR: ~3–4% (up to 8% in patients with low EF) - AVR+CABG: 6% - AVR in octogenarians: ~6–9%
    Lesson 11.105.19: Multiple valve replacement: 9%
    Lesson 11.105.20: Redo valve replacement

    Lesson 12.22.1: f antibiotics.
    Lesson 12.22.2: in about 50% of patients-.
    Lesson 12.22.3: Empirical antimicrobial therapy: The initial choice of empirical treatment depends on
    Lesson 12.22.4: Previous antibiotic therapy.
    Lesson 12.22.5: IE in a native valve or a prosthesis (and if so, when surgery was performed [early vs. late PVE]).
    Lesson 12.22.6: The place of the infection (community, nosocomial, or non-nosocomial healthcare-associated IE).
    Lesson 12.22.7: Local epidemiology, especially for antibiotic resistance and culture-negative pathogens.
    Lesson 12.22.8: Duration of antimicrobial therapy
    Lesson 12.22.9: Drug treatment of PVE should last longer (≥ 6 weeks) than that of NVE (2-6 weeks).
    Lesson 12.22.10: Different strategies between NVE and PVE
    Lesson 12.22.11: NVE and late PVE regimens should cover staphylococci, streptococci, and enterococci.
    Lesson 12.22.12: Coagulase negative staph. should be empirically covered in PVE but not in NVE.
    Lesson 12.22.13: In staphylococcal PVE, the regimen should include rifampin whenever the strain is susceptible.
    Lesson 12.22.14: Notes regarding specific antibiotics

    Lesson 12.25.8: Blood culture-negative IE

    Lesson 13.5.5: The arthritis of rheumatic fever responds promptly to NSAIDs within 48 hrs.
    Lesson 13.5.6: Radiographs may show features of a joint effusion, but no other abnormalities are noted.
    Lesson 13.5.7: Carditis
    Lesson 13.5.8: Sydenham Chorea
    Lesson 13.5.9: Chorea may be the only presenting manifestation of rheumatic fever. It is more common in females.
    Lesson 13.5.10: It usually lasts for 8 to 15 weeks (may last for 1 week to 2 years).
    Lesson 13.5.11: Chorea does not occur simultaneously with arthritis but may coexist with carditis.
    Lesson 13.5.12: Subcutaneous Nodules
    Lesson 13.5.13: Erythema Marginatum

    Lesson 13.5.19: Blood culture, if febrile
    Lesson 13.5.20: ECG: the most common finding is PR prolongation and sinus tachycardia.
    Lesson 13.5.21: Chest radiograph
    Lesson 13.5.22: Echocardiogram
    Lesson 13.5.23: Tests for Alternative Diagnoses, Depending on Clinical Features
    Lesson 13.5.24: Repeated blood cultures with temperature spikes if infective endocarditis is suspected

    Lesson 13.5.27: Serology and autoimmune markers for arboviral, autoimmune, or reactive arthritis

    Lesson 14.10.1: DD of a wide QRS complex tachycardia includes
    Lesson 14.10.2: Hyperkalemia;
    Lesson 14.10.3: Drug toxicity (class I antiarrhythmic agents, tricyclics);
    Lesson 14.10.4: Ventricular pacemaker tracking an atrial arrhythmia (lack of mode switch), or
    Lesson 14.10.5: Pacemaker‐ mediated tachycardia.
    Lesson 14.10.6: Features characteristic of VT versus SVT with aberrancy
    Lesson 14.10.7: QRS morphology inconsistent with a typical RBBB or LBBB.
    Lesson 14.10.8: Onset of R‐to‐nadir of S > 100 ms in any precordial lead.
    Lesson 14.10.9: QRS complexes outnumber P waves (with AV association or AV dissociation).
    Lesson 14.10.10: Deep Q wave in V4–V6, is particularly suggestive of VT.
    Lesson 14.10.11: A tachycardia that starts with a PVC and has a morphology similar to this PVC is VT.
    Lesson 14.10.12: In VT, the QRS complex is wide in its initial portion and has a slow initial upslope or downslope.
  Mini Chapter 14.11: Characters supportive of SVT
    Lesson 14.11.1: f the terminal QRS portion.
  Mini Chapter 14.12: AF with pre-excitation is diagnosed when
    Lesson 14.12.1: AF has VT morphological features; or
    Lesson 14.12.2: AF is wide and polymorphic (QRS varies in height and width), bizarre looking, or
    Lesson 14.12.3: AF is very fast (> 200 bpm).
    Lesson 14.12.4: Absence of RS in precordial leads
    Lesson 14.12.5: RS >100 ms in any lead (1)
    Lesson 14.12.6: Initial R wave
    Lesson 14.12.7: Initial R or Q wave > 40 ms
    Lesson 14.12.8: Presence of a notch of a predominantly
    Lesson 14.12.9: Lead V1: Monophasic R, Rsr’, biphasic qR
    Lesson 14.12.10: Lead V6: R:S ratio < 1 (rS, QS patterns)
    Lesson 14.12.11: Lead V1: Broad R wave, slurred or notched-down
    Lesson 14.12.12: Lead V6: Q or QS wave
  Mini Chapter 14.13: Any of the following criteria favors VT
    Lesson 14.13.1: monophasic R or qR in V1
    Lesson 14.13.2: R taller then R’
    Lesson 14.13.3: rS in V6
    Lesson 14.13.4: Initial R > 40 msec
    Lesson 14.13.5: Slurred or notched S in V1
    Lesson 14.13.6: r V2
    Lesson 14.13.7: Beginning of Q or QS in V6
    Lesson 14.13.8: nset and predominantly

Chapter 17: Ventricular Arrhythmia
  Mini Chapter 17.1: Definitions and Classification
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 17.2: Ventricular arrhythmia subtypes
    Lesson 17.2.1: Unifocal or monomorphic PVCs: PVCs with a single QRS morphology.
    Lesson 17.2.2: Multifocal, multiform, or polymorphic PVCs: PVCs with different QRS morphologies.
    Lesson 17.2.3: Short-coupled PVC: A PVC that interrupts the T-wave of the preceding conducted beat.
  Mini Chapter 17.3: VT can be classified according to duration
    Lesson 17.3.1: Non-sustained VT (NSVT): Run of consecutive ventricular beats persisting for 3 beats to 30 s.
    Lesson 17.3.2: Sustained VT: Continuous VT for at least 30 s, or which requires an intervention for termination.
  Mini Chapter 17.4: VT can be classified according to morphology
    Lesson 17.4.1: Monomorphic VT (MVT): Same QRS morphology from beat to beat.
    Lesson 17.4.2: Polymorphic VT (PVT): is a VT that has two or more QRS morphologies.
    Lesson 17.4.3: Sudden Cardiac death
    Lesson 17.4.4: Syncope
    Lesson 17.4.5: Pathogenic variant: genetic variants most likely to cause an associated disease.
    Lesson 17.4.6: Site of origin of the PVC: (PVC looks away, i.e., looks negative, from where it originates)
    Lesson 17.4.7: Right vs. left
    Lesson 17.4.8: RV origin → LBBB morphology
    Lesson 17.4.9: LV origin → RBBB morphology
    Lesson 17.4.10: Anterior vs. inferior
    Lesson 17.4.11: Anterior origin → QRS (+) in the inferior leads (vertical axis).
    Lesson 17.4.12: Inferior origin → QRS (–) in the inferior leads (left axis).
    Lesson 17.4.13: Basal vs. apical
    Lesson 17.4.14: Basal origin → QRS is upright in all precordial leads, and (–) in I-aVL if lateral origin.
    Lesson 17.4.15: Apical origin → QRS is negative in all precordial leads, as it looks away from them, esp. V3-V6.

Chapter 18: Bradyarrhythmia
  Mini Chapter 18.1: Classification
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 18.2: Sinus Node Disorders
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 18.3: AV junction disorders
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 18.4: Intraventricular conduction disturbance
    Lesson 18.4.1: qRs in I, aVL and V4-V6 with slurring in ‘s’ wave.
    Lesson 18.4.2: QR in aVR with slurring in R, and negative T wave
    Lesson 18.4.3: Partial RBBB: QRS width < 120 ms. ECG features include
    Lesson 18.4.4: rSr’ in V1. The r’ is not wide and may have a variable voltage.
    Lesson 18.4.5: terminal, not wide, ‘r’ in aVR, and ‘s’ in I and V6.
  Mini Chapter 18.5: LBBB
    Lesson 18.5.1: Advanced LBBB: QRS ≥ 120 ms. ECG features include
    Lesson 18.5.2: QS or rS in V1 with small ‘r’ with ST slightly elevated and positive asymmetrical T wave.
    Lesson 18.5.3: Exclusive R wave in I and aVL often with a negative asymmetrical T wave, slight ST depression, and
    Lesson 18.5.4: Usually, QS in aVR with positive T wave.
    Lesson 18.5.5: Generally, the ST segment is slightly opposed to the QRS polarity.
    Lesson 18.5.6: Partial LBBB: QRS < 120 ms. ECG features include
    Lesson 18.5.7: r LBBB are not present.
    Lesson 18.5.8: Leftwards QRS axis between -45 and -75.
    Lesson 18.5.9: S until V6 with IDT in V6 less than IDT in aVL, and with IDT in aVL ≥ 50 ms.
    Lesson 18.5.10: QRS duration < 120 ms. However, in isolated cases the QRS may not surpass 100 ms.
    Lesson 18.5.11: In advanced cases, mid-terminal slurring is present in I and aVL.
  Mini Chapter 18.6: Left posterior fascicular block (Inferoposterior hemiblock)
    Lesson 18.6.1: QRS axis greatly deviated to the right (between +90 and +140).
    Lesson 18.6.2: rS in I and aVL, and qR in II, III, and aVF.
    Lesson 18.6.3: QRS width < 120 ms.
    Lesson 18.6.4: IDT ≥ 50 ms in aVF and V6, with IDT < 50 ms in aVL.
    Lesson 18.6.5: Mid-terminal slurring in II, III, and aVF in advanced cases (in the absence of partial RBBB).
  Mini Chapter 18.7: Bifascicular block implies a block in two of the three conduction fascicles, such as
    Lesson 18.7.1: LBBB
    Lesson 18.7.2: or RBBB + LAFB (~ RBBB with left axis)
    Lesson 18.7.3: or RBBB + LPFB (~ RBBB with right axis, which may also be RBBB + RVH).
    Lesson 18.7.4: RBBB with alternating LAFB and LPFB.
  Mini Chapter 18.8: Outside the pause that follows an obvious PAC or PVC, a pause on a rhythm strip may be secondary to
    (treated as Lesson itself)

Chapter 20: Syncope
  Mini Chapter 20.1: Definition of syncope
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 20.2: Classification of syncope
    Lesson 20.2.1: Vasovagal syncope
    Lesson 20.2.2: Situational syncope
    Lesson 20.2.3: Carotid sinus syndrome
    Lesson 20.2.4: Post-exertional syncope
    Lesson 20.2.5: Postural orthostatic tachycardia syndrome (POTS)
    Lesson 20.2.6: utflow. BP drops with the reduction in vascular resistance.
    Lesson 20.2.7: Ventricular tachycardia secondary to structural heart disease, or primary electrical disease.
  Mini Chapter 20.3: Pathophysiology
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 20.4: Initial Evaluation
    Lesson 20.4.1: The initial evaluation should answer key questions
    Lesson 20.4.2: Unconsciousness often lasts longer than 5 min.
    Lesson 20.4.3: Postictal confusion or paralysis
    Lesson 20.4.4: Prolonged tonic-clonic movements.
    Lesson 20.4.5: Tongue biting strongly suggests seizure.
  Mini Chapter 20.5: Initial syncope evaluation include
    Lesson 20.5.1: Additional evaluation
    Lesson 20.5.2: Immediate ECG monitoring when there is a suspicion of arrhythmic syncope.
    Lesson 20.5.3: r any history of stroke.
    Lesson 20.5.4: Head-up tilt testing when there is suspicion of syncope due to OH or reflex syncope.
  Mini Chapter 20.6: Clinical clues
    Lesson 20.6.1: Position during syncope
    Lesson 20.6.2: Supine → reflex syncope is unlikely
    Lesson 20.6.3: Sitting or standing position → any cause is possible.
    Lesson 20.6.4: Within a few minutes of standing → orthostatic hypotension
    Lesson 20.6.5: Prolonged sitting or standing → vasovagal
    Lesson 20.6.6: Situation during syncope
    Lesson 20.6.7: Post-exertion → vasovagal
    Lesson 20.6.8: Postprandial → orthostatic hypotension
    Lesson 20.6.9: Sudden fear, pain, unpleasant sight, hot environment → vasovagal
    Lesson 20.6.10: Strain situation (micturition) → reflex syncope
    Lesson 20.6.11: Head turning, shaving, tight collar → carotid sinus syndrome.
    Lesson 20.6.12: Prodromes (abdominal discomfort, malaise, palpitations, nausea, blurry vision)
    Lesson 20.6.13: Yes and > 5 seconds → reflex syncope.
    Lesson 20.6.14: How consciousness is regained after syncope?
    Lesson 20.6.15: Promptly → cardiac syncope
    Lesson 20.6.16: Prolonged fatigue or nausea after syncope → reflex syncope
    Lesson 20.6.17: Confusion → seizure
    Lesson 20.6.18: Color during syncope
    Lesson 20.6.19: Pale, diaphoresis → reflex syncope, orthostatic syncope
    Lesson 20.6.20: Blue → arrhythmia, seizure
    Lesson 20.6.21: Duration
    Lesson 20.6.22: > 5 minutes → seizure, hypoglycemia; not syncope (except occasionally aortic stenosis)
    Lesson 20.6.23: Underlying heart disease, chest pain → cardiac syncope
    Lesson 20.6.24: Multiple neuropsychiatric or blood pressure medications → reflex or orthostatic syncope
    Lesson 20.6.25: The presence or the lack of injury does not help differentiate cardiac from reflex syncope.
  Mini Chapter 20.7: ECG or rhythm monitor findings suggestive of cardiac syncope
    Lesson 20.7.1: Bradyarrhythmia-related syncope is established with any of the following
    Lesson 20.7.2: Mobitz II or complete AV block.
    Lesson 20.7.3: Alternating LBBB or RBBB (on the same ECG or on ECGs obtained on separate occasions)
    Lesson 20.7.4: Bradyarrhythmia-related syncope is suggested with
    Lesson 20.7.5: Isolated RBBB or LBBB (VT also possible, depending on the underlying cardiac disease)
    Lesson 20.7.6: Mobitz type I AV block
    Lesson 20.7.7: Tachyarrhythmia-related syncope is established with: Sustained VT or fast SVT (> 160 bpm)
    Lesson 20.7.8: Underlying heart disease and VT are suggested with
    Lesson 20.7.9: Q waves
    Lesson 20.7.10: LBBB, RBBB, QRS > 110 ms
    Lesson 20.7.11: LVH, RVH, large R wave in V1
    Lesson 20.7.12: r epsilon waves (ARVD)
    Lesson 20.7.13: Acute ST–T abnormalities
  Mini Chapter 20.8: Reflex syncope and OH
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 20.9: Cardiac syncope
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 20.10: Arrhythmic syncope is highly probable when the ECG shows
    Lesson 20.10.1: Persistent sinus bradycardia < 40 b.p.m. or sinus pauses > 3 s in awake state and in absence of
    Lesson 20.10.2: Mobitz II second- and third-degree AV block.
    Lesson 20.10.3: Alternating left and right BBB.
    Lesson 20.10.4: VT or rapid paroxysmal SVT.
    Lesson 20.10.5: Non-sustained episodes of polymorphic VT and long or short QT interval; or
    Lesson 20.10.6: Pacemaker or ICD malfunction with cardiac pauses.
  Mini Chapter 20.11: Syncopal events
    Lesson 20.11.1: Major
    Lesson 20.11.2: New onset of chest discomfort, breathlessness, abdominal pain, or headache
    Lesson 20.11.3: Syncope during exertion or when supine
    Lesson 20.11.4: Sudden onset palpitation followed by syncope
    Lesson 20.11.5: Minor (high risk only if associated with structural heart disease or abnormal ECG)
    Lesson 20.11.6: No warning symptoms or short (< 10s) prodrome
    Lesson 20.11.7: Family history of SCD at young age
    Lesson 20.11.8: Syncope in the sitting position
  Mini Chapter 20.12: Past medical history
    Lesson 20.12.1: Severe structural or coronary artey disease (heart failure, Low EF or previous MI)
  Mini Chapter 20.13: Physical Examination
    Lesson 20.13.1: Unexplained systolic blood pressure in the ED < 90 mmHg
    Lesson 20.13.2: Suggestion of GI bleed on rectal examinations
    Lesson 20.13.3: Persistent bradycardia (< 40 b.p.m) in awake state and in absence of physical training
    Lesson 20.13.4: Undiagnosed systolic murmur
  Mini Chapter 20.14: ECG
    Lesson 20.14.1: ECG changes consistent with acute ischemia
    Lesson 20.14.2: Mobitz II second- and third- degree AV block
    Lesson 20.14.3: Slow AF (< 40 b.p.m)
    Lesson 20.14.4: Persistent sinus bradycardia (< 40 b.p.m) or
    Lesson 20.14.5: Bundle branch block, intraventricular conduction
    Lesson 20.14.6: r cardiomyopathy
    Lesson 20.14.7: Sustained and non-sustained VT
    Lesson 20.14.8: Dysfunction of pacemaker or ICD
    Lesson 20.14.9: Type 1 burgada pattern
    Lesson 20.14.10: QTc > 460 ms in repeated ECGs indicating LQTs.
    Lesson 20.14.11: Risk Stratification
    Lesson 20.14.12: Mobitz I second degree AV block and first-degree
    Lesson 20.14.13: Asymptomatic inappropriate mild sinus
    Lesson 20.14.14: Paroxysmal SVT or AF
    Lesson 20.14.15: Pre-exicted QRS complex
    Lesson 20.14.16: Short QTc interval (≤ 340 ms)
    Lesson 20.14.17: Atypical Burgada patterns
    Lesson 20.14.18: Negative T wave in right precordial leads, epsilon
    Lesson 20.14.19: f syncope.
    Lesson 20.14.20: Criteria for hospital admission
    Lesson 20.14.21: r syncope due to OH, are discharged from the ED.
  Mini Chapter 20.15: Additional advice and clinical perspectives
    Lesson 20.15.1: In the ED, presyncope should be managed as syncope; as it carries the same prognosis.
    Lesson 20.15.2: Diagnostic radiology and laboratory tests such as chest X-ray, brain CT, routine blood hematology,
  Mini Chapter 20.16: Additional Diagnostic tests
    Lesson 20.16.1: Carotid sinus massage
    Lesson 20.16.2: An abnormal response to carotid sinus massage is defined as any of the following
    Lesson 20.16.3: Vasodepressor response: SBP decreases by ≥ 50 mmHg and/or
    Lesson 20.16.4: Cardioinhibitory response: pause ≥ 3 sec (sinus pause or AV block).
    Lesson 20.16.5: f older populations and should be used with caution for diagnosis of the mechanism of syncope.

Chapter 21: Pericardial diseases
  Mini Chapter 21.1: In all cases of suspected pericardial disease, a first diagnostic evaluation is recommended with
    Lesson 21.1.1: Auscultation
    Lesson 21.1.2: Transthoracic echocardiography
    Lesson 21.1.3: Chest X-ray
  Mini Chapter 21.2: Major factors include
    Lesson 21.2.1: fever
    Lesson 21.2.2: subacute course (symptoms developing over several days or weeks)
    Lesson 21.2.3: large pericardial effusion (diastolic echo-free space >20 mm in width)
    Lesson 21.2.4: cardiac tamponade
    Lesson 21.2.5: failure of aspirin or NSAIDs
  Mini Chapter 21.3: Aetiology
    Lesson 21.3.1: The four most common causes are
    Lesson 21.3.2: Other common causes of pericarditis, occurring in specific contexts
  Mini Chapter 21.4: Infectious causes
    Lesson 21.4.1: Parasitic (very rare): Echinococcus spp, Toxoplasma spp
  Mini Chapter 21.5: Non-infectious causes
    Lesson 21.5.1: Neoplastic
    Lesson 21.5.2: Metabolic: Uraemia, myxoedema, anorexia nervosa, other rare.
  Mini Chapter 21.6: Traumatic
    Lesson 21.6.1: Early onset (rare)
    Lesson 21.6.2: Direct injury (penetrating thoracic injury, esophageal perforation).
    Lesson 21.6.3: Indirect injury (non-penetrating thoracic injury, radiation injury).
    Lesson 21.6.4: Delayed onset: Pericardial injury syndromes (common; such as postmyocardial infarction syndrome,
  Mini Chapter 21.7: Drug-related
    Lesson 21.7.1: Lupus-like syndrome (procainamide, hydralazine, methyldopa, isoniazid, phenytoin).
    Lesson 21.7.2: Penicillins as a hypersensitivity pericarditis with eosinophilia.
  Mini Chapter 21.8: Others
    Lesson 21.8.1: Common: Amyloidosis, Aortic dissection, PAH and HF.
    Lesson 21.8.2: Uncommon: congenital partial and complete absence of the pericardium.
  Mini Chapter 21.9: Predictors of poor prognosis
    Lesson 21.9.1: Major
    Lesson 21.9.2: High fever > 38 C.
    Lesson 21.9.3: Subacute course (symptoms over several days without a clear-cut acute onset).
    Lesson 21.9.4: Large pericardial effusion (i.e. diastolic echo-free space > 20 mm).
    Lesson 21.9.5: Cardiac tamponade.
    Lesson 21.9.6: Failure to respond within 7 days to NSAIDs.
    Lesson 21.9.7: Minor
    Lesson 21.9.8: Myopericarditis.
    Lesson 21.9.9: Immunodepression
    Lesson 21.9.10: Trauma
    Lesson 21.9.11: Oral anticoagulant therapy.
  Mini Chapter 21.10: Types
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 21.11: Additional supporting findings
    Lesson 21.11.1: Elevation of markers of inflammation (i.e CRP, ESR and WBCs)
    Lesson 21.11.2: Evidence of pericardial inflammation by an imaging technique (CT, CMR)
  Mini Chapter 21.12: Diagnosis
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 21.13: The diagnosis of pericarditis requires two of the following four features
    Lesson 21.13.1: The rub is due to the friction of the inflamed visceral and parietal pericardial layers.
    Lesson 21.13.2: Low QRS voltage and QRS alternans
    Lesson 21.13.3: ther‐beat alternation of two different QRS morphologies).
    Lesson 21.13.4: Most often, No effusion is present (“dry” pericarditis).
  Mini Chapter 21.14: Additional supporting findings
    Lesson 21.14.1: Evidence of pericardial inflammation by an imaging technique (CT, MRI).
  Mini Chapter 21.15: Management
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 21.16: Prognosis
    Lesson 21.16.1: Pericarditis is a self‐limiting disease with no complication or recurrence in > 70 % of patients.
    Lesson 21.16.2: Low (<1%) for idiopathic and presumed viral pericarditis;
    Lesson 21.16.3: Intermediate (2 –5%) for autoimmune, immunemediated and neoplastic aetiologies; and
    Lesson 21.16.4: High (20–30%) for bacterial aetiologies, especially with TB and purulent pericarditis.
    Lesson 21.16.5: One‐third of patients have pleuropericardial involvement during these recurrences.
  Mini Chapter 21.17: Causes
    Lesson 21.17.1: Similarly to acute pericarditis, the 5 most common causes of a moderate or large effusion are
    Lesson 21.17.2: f effusion and tamponade. Approximately 30–50% of large pericardial effusions are viral/idiopathic.
    Lesson 21.17.3: Other causes are seen in specific contexts

    Lesson 22.113.6: Coronary artery occlusion.

    Lesson 22.113.10: Systemic RV dysfunction and failure.

    Lesson 22.116.1: Complications: Common complications are
    Lesson 22.116.2: Stenosis or regurgitation of the valved conduit between the RV and the PA.
    Lesson 22.116.3: LVOT obstruction (i.e., obstruction of the flow from the LV to the aorta).
    Lesson 22.116.4: Residual VSD.
    Lesson 22.116.5: LV dysfunction.
    Lesson 22.116.6: Arrhythmias, both ventricular and supraventricular.
    Lesson 22.116.7: Endocarditis of the valved conduit.
    Lesson 22.116.8: Death, either sudden (arrhythmia) or due to heart failure.
  Mini Chapter 22.117: Follow-Up
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.118: Pathophysiology
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.119: Natural history
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.120: Clinical Picture
    Lesson 22.120.1: ccur.
  Mini Chapter 22.121: Diagnostic work-up
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.122: Management
    Lesson 22.122.1: Surgical
    Lesson 22.122.2: Initial treatment: this will depend on the mode of presentation and the associated lesions
    Lesson 22.122.3: Neonates with arch hypoplasia/coarctation require resuscitation and urgent surgical repair.
    Lesson 22.122.4: Patients with pulmonary stenosis or atresia require initial palliation with a BT shunt.
    Lesson 22.122.5: Definitive Procedures: There are two major approaches to surgical management of L-TGA
  Mini Chapter 22.123: Follow-up recommendations
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.124: Late complications are
    Lesson 22.124.1: Systemic RV dysfunction and failure.
    Lesson 22.124.2: Progressive TR (systemic AV valve).
    Lesson 22.124.3: LVOT Obstruction.
    Lesson 22.124.4: ccur during pregnancy.
    Lesson 22.124.5: VTs (extremely rare).
    Lesson 22.124.6: Absent RVOT (e.g., pulmonary atresia, truncus arteriosus).
    Lesson 22.124.7: Unsuitable RVOT (e.g., D-TGA + VSD + PS or complicated DORV or L-TGA +PS).
    Lesson 22.124.8: Iatrogenic: e.g., Ross operation.
    Lesson 22.124.9: There is no ideal conduit. Limited durability implicates early reoperation.
    Lesson 22.124.10: Freedom from reoperation for conduit failure at 20 years was reported at 32% and 40%.
  Mini Chapter 22.125: Diagnostic work-up
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.126: Indications for intervention
    Lesson 22.126.1: f the following criteria is present
    Lesson 22.126.2: Decrease in objective exercise capacity (CPET).
    Lesson 22.126.3: Progressive RV dilation to RVESVi ≥ 80 mL/m2, and/or RVEDVi ≥ 160 mL/m2, and/or
    Lesson 22.126.4: Progressive RV systolic dysfunction.
    Lesson 22.126.5: RVSP > 80 mmHg
  Mini Chapter 22.127: Follow-up recommendations
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.128: Pathophysiology
    Lesson 22.128.1: In doubly committed VSD or Non committed VSD: mild cyanosis is present and the PBF is increased.
  Mini Chapter 22.129: Natural history
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.130: Clinical Picture
    Lesson 22.130.1: Subaortic VSD without PS
    Lesson 22.130.2: Physical findings resemble those of a large VSD (i.e CHF and failure to thrive).
    Lesson 22.130.3: Chest radiographs show cardiomegaly with increased PVMs and prominent MPA.
    Lesson 22.130.4: Subaortic VSD with PS (Fallot type)
    Lesson 22.130.5: Physical findings resemble those of TOF.
    Lesson 22.130.6: ECG shows RAD, RAH, and RVH or RBBB.
    Lesson 22.130.7: Chest radiographs show normal heart size (with upturned apex) and decreased PVMs.
    Lesson 22.130.8: Subpulmonary VSD (Taussig-Bing malformation)
    Lesson 22.130.9: Physical findings resemble those of TGA with severe cyanosis in newborn infants.
    Lesson 22.130.10: ECG shows RAD, RAH, and RVH. First-degree AV block is frequently present.
    Lesson 22.130.11: Chest radiographs show cardiomegaly with increased pulmonary vascular markings and prominent MPA.
  Mini Chapter 22.131: Diagnostic work-up
    Lesson 22.131.1: The size and position of the VSD should be determined in relation to the great arteries
    Lesson 22.131.2: Typical subpulmonary or subaortic VSD can be demonstrated by parasternal long-axis.
    Lesson 22.131.3: Doubly committed VSD is recognized in the parasternal or the apical long-axis view.
    Lesson 22.131.4: Non-committed VSDs are best seen in the apical four-chamber view.
  Mini Chapter 22.132: Management
    Lesson 22.132.1: Surgical
    Lesson 22.132.2: For non-committed or multiple VSDs (with large PBF and CHF)
    Lesson 22.132.3: Palliative procedure: a PA banding is occasionally performed.
    Lesson 22.132.4: For subaortic VSD and PS (Fallot type)
    Lesson 22.132.5: Palliative procedure: a B-T shunt or RVOT stent may be indicated to increase pulmonary blood flow.
    Lesson 22.132.6: For subpulmonary VSD (Taussig-Bing malformation)
  Mini Chapter 22.133: Follow-up recommendations
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.134: Pathophysiology
    Lesson 22.134.1: f PBF
  Mini Chapter 22.135: Natural history
    Lesson 22.135.1: f CHF.
    Lesson 22.135.2: In patients with associated PS: cyanosis increases if PS worsens.
    Lesson 22.135.3: Progressive AV valve regurgitation is poorly tolerated.
    Lesson 22.135.4: Complete heart block develops in about 12% of patients.
    Lesson 22.135.5: The cause of death can be CHF, arrhythmias, or sudden death.
  Mini Chapter 22.136: Univentricular heart includes the following malformations
    Lesson 22.136.1: Tricuspid atresia
    Lesson 22.136.2: Double-inlet left ventricle (DILV)
    Lesson 22.136.3: Pulmonary atresia with intact septum
    Lesson 22.136.4: Unbalanced AVSD (dominant left)
    Lesson 22.136.5: Transposition/VSD with small RV
    Lesson 22.136.6: Hypoplastic left heart syndrome
    Lesson 22.136.7: DORV with mitral atresia
    Lesson 22.136.8: Unbalanced AVSD (dominant right)
  Mini Chapter 22.137: Diagnostic work-up
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.138: Fundamental TTE parameters/issues/items in the diagnosis of UVHs are
    Lesson 22.138.1: Abdominal and atrial situs.
    Lesson 22.138.2: Position of the heart in the chest and position of the apex.
    Lesson 22.138.3: Veno-atrial, AV, and ventriculo-arterial connections.
    Lesson 22.138.4: bstruction towards the aorta or pulmonary vascular bed.
    Lesson 22.138.5: AV valve function should be evaluated, with special focus on regurgitation.
    Lesson 22.138.6: Ventricular function/hypertrophy.
    Lesson 22.138.7: ASD/VSD type, size, number, location.
    Lesson 22.138.8: Ascending aorta, aortic arch, and descending aorta; detect/ exclude coarctation.
    Lesson 22.138.9: PAs common trunk, branches, and sources of pulmonary blood supply.
    Lesson 22.138.10: Visualization of shunts (BlalockTaussig, Waterston, etc.).
  Mini Chapter 22.139: Management
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 22.140: Idea
    Lesson 22.140.1: Ideal candidates for Fontan are those who have normal LV function and low PVR
    Lesson 22.140.2: Normal LV function results from prevention of excessive volume or pressure loading of the LV by
    Lesson 22.140.3: Preventing excessive volume load by using a relatively small systemic-to-pulmonary shunt.
    Lesson 22.140.4: Avoiding ventricular hypertrophy (e.g., by relieving the LVOT obstruction)
    Lesson 22.140.5: Low pulmonary vascular resistance may result from
  Mini Chapter 22.141: Strategy

    Lesson 22.141.4: Second-stage surgical palliative procedures
    Lesson 22.141.5: peration. Alternatively, the hemi-Fontan procedure can be performed.
    Lesson 22.141.6: r PAP, should be considered for a bidirectional Glenn shunt.

  Mini Chapter 22.155: Clinical Picture

  Mini Chapter 22.156: Diagnostic Workup
    Lesson 22.156.1: Medical
    Lesson 22.156.2: The patient should be intubated and ventilated, and metabolic acidosis should be corrected.
    Lesson 22.156.3: An intravenous infusion of PGE1 may produce temporary improvement.
    Lesson 22.156.4: Balloon atrial septostomy may help decompress the LA and temporarily improve oxygenation.
    Lesson 22.156.5: Stage I: One of the following procedures is done in preparation for a future Fontan operation.
    Lesson 22.156.6: Stage II (at 3 to 6 mo): Bidirectional Glenn (BDG) or hemi-Fontan operation
    Lesson 22.156.7: Perform cardiac catheterization (to find and occlude venous collaterals) or
    Lesson 22.156.8: Proceed with Fontan operation
    Lesson 22.156.9: Stage III (Fontan operation) within 1-2 yrs after stage II operation.

    Lesson 23.32.18: Continue anticoagulation for ≥ 3 months and schedule the patient for a 3-month follow-up visit.
    Lesson 23.32.19: At the 3-month follow-up visit, assess the presence of persisting or worsening symptoms, or
    Lesson 23.32.20: If patient was taking an oestrogen-containing contraceptive, and especially if PE occurred in
    Lesson 23.32.21: All other cases
    Lesson 23.32.22: Manage chronic anticoagulation as after acute PE occurring in the absence of identifiable risk
    Lesson 23.32.23: Consider using a validated prediction model for quantification of the risk for VTE recurrence; for
    Lesson 23.32.24: Advise patient on the need for prophylaxis with LMWH in case of pregnancy.
    Lesson 23.32.25: Anticoagulation with LMWH throughout pregnancy and > 6 weeks post-partum.
    Lesson 23.32.26: No NOACs during pregnancy or lactation.
    Lesson 23.32.27: Advise patient on the need for prophylaxis with LMWH in future pregnancies.

    Lesson 23.32.29: If cancer in remission

    Lesson 23.32.31: In either case, periodically reassess the risk benefit ratio of continuing anticoagulation.

    Lesson 24.31.3: The addition of riociguat to bosentan should be considered.

  Mini Chapter 25.1: The aortic wall is composed histologically of three layers
    Lesson 25.1.1: Thin inner tunica intima lined by the endothelium;
    Lesson 25.1.2: The outer tunica adventitia containing mainly collagen, vasa vasorum, and lymphatics.
    Lesson 25.1.3: f aortic expansion is about 0.9 mm in men and 0.7 mm in women for each decade of life.

    Lesson 25.49.1: Diminished or absent pulses with limb claudications (in 84-96%), vascular bruits (80-94%).
    Lesson 25.49.2: Hypertension (33-83%) usually reflecting renal artery stenosis.
    Lesson 25.49.3: Congestive heart failure associated with hypertension and aortic regurgitation.
    Lesson 25.49.4: Takayasu retinopathy (37%).
    Lesson 25.49.5: Pulmonary artery involvement (14-100%).
    Lesson 25.49.6: Role of imaging
    Lesson 25.49.7: Angiography remains the gold standard for confirming the diagnosis and planning the treatment.
    Lesson 25.49.8: Treatment
    Lesson 25.49.9: Leiosarcomas and fibrosarcomas originate from the media or adventitia of the aortic wall.
    Lesson 25.49.10: Diagnosis and workup
    Lesson 25.49.11: cclusion of the mesenteric or peripheral artery.

    Lesson 27.58.19: Reduction of hospitalization (28%)
    Lesson 27.58.20: Improvement of symptoms and peak oxygen consumption (20%)
    Lesson 27.58.21: Small but significant improvement in cardiac size.

    Lesson 27.58.23: Small improvement of cardiac performance.
    Lesson 27.58.24: Reduction (25 to 32%) in circulating levels of angiotensin Il and aldosterone.
    Lesson 27.58.25: Improvement of exercise performance and ventilation dynamics.
    Lesson 27.58.26: Exercise therapy after cardiac surgery

    Lesson 28.18.8: Relative Wall Thickness (RWT)= 2 x LV posterior wall thickness/LVIDD. Normal value: ≤ 0.42

    Lesson 28.18.13: Concentric remodelling: RWT > 0.42, normal LVMI
    Lesson 28.18.14: Concentric hypertrophy: RWT > 0.42, increased LVMI

Chapter 30: Cardiodiabetes
  Mini Chapter 30.1: Diagnosis of diabetes
    Lesson 30.1.1: HbA1C ≥ 6.5% or
    Lesson 30.1.2: Fasting plasma glucose (FPG) ≥ 126 mg/dl (1) or
    Lesson 30.1.3: Symptoms of hyperglycemia plus non fasting plasma glucose of ≥ 200 mg/dl.
    Lesson 30.1.4: The diagnosis of Pre-diabetes depends on the detection of any of the following (WHO criteria)
    Lesson 30.1.5: HbA1C 6-6.4% (1) or
    Lesson 30.1.6: Fasting plasma glucose of 110-125 mg/dl (Impaired fasting glucose [IFG]) (2) or
    Lesson 30.1.7: 2-hr plasma glucose of 140-200 mg/dl with FPG < 126 mg/dL (Impaired glucose tolerance [IGT]).
    Lesson 30.1.8: Two tests of either FPG or random glucose levels in the diabetes range.
  Mini Chapter 30.2: Screening for diabetes
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 30.3: Classifying diabetes
    Lesson 30.3.1: Type 1 diabetes
    Lesson 30.3.2: Type 2 diabetes
    Lesson 30.3.3: Monogenic diabetes
  Mini Chapter 30.4: CV manifestations of diabetes
    Lesson 30.4.1: Heart failure
    Lesson 30.4.2: Increasing fasting glucose by 1 mmol/L increases the risk of heart failure by 14%.
    Lesson 30.4.3: vertime.
  Mini Chapter 30.5: Possible contributing mechanisms include
    Lesson 30.5.1: Collagen accumulation leading to decreased myocardial compliance.
    Lesson 30.5.2: Altered energy substrate supply and utilization
    Lesson 30.5.3: Oxidative stress
    Lesson 30.5.4: Abnormal myocardial calcium handling
    Lesson 30.5.5: Endothelial dysfunction
    Lesson 30.5.6: Deposition of intramyocardial fat (myocardial steatosis)
    Lesson 30.5.7: Cardiac autonomic neuropathy
    Lesson 30.5.8: Gene abnormalities
    Lesson 30.5.9: Thrombosis and coagulation
    Lesson 30.5.10: Higher prevalence of other complications such as diabetic nephropathy
    Lesson 30.5.11: Decreased perception of myocardial ischaemia
    Lesson 30.5.12: Resting tachycardia due to parasympathetic denervation which increases myocardial O2 demands
    Lesson 30.5.13: Abnormal coronary blood flow regulation
    Lesson 30.5.14: Increased prevalence of QT prolongation and QT dispersion
  Mini Chapter 30.6: CV risk assessment in patients with diabetes and prediabetes
    Lesson 30.6.1: eGFR < 45 mL/min/1.73 m2 irrespective of albuminuria or
    Lesson 30.6.2: Proteinuria (UACR > 300 mg/g; stage A3) or
    Lesson 30.6.3: Very high CV risk: Patients with T2DM with
    Lesson 30.6.4: Clinically established ASCVD or
    Lesson 30.6.5: Severe TOD or
    Lesson 30.6.6: 10-year CVD risk ≥ 20% using SCORE2-Diabetes.

  Mini Chapter 31.39: Two major mechanisms underlie perioperative MI
    Lesson 31.39.1: Atrial fibrillation
    Lesson 31.39.2: Peri-operative stroke
    Lesson 31.39.3: Peri-operative stroke is mainly ischaemic or cardioembolic, and AF is often the leading condition.
    Lesson 31.39.4: The associated mortality ranges from 18- 26%.
    Lesson 31.39.5: In an attempt to attenuate the risk of peri-operative stroke
    Lesson 31.39.6: Adequate selection of the anaesthetic technique (regional vs. neuraxial vs. general anaesthesia),
    Lesson 31.39.7: Prevention and treatment of AF,
    Lesson 31.39.8: Euglycaemic control (avoiding both hyperglycaemia and hypoglycaemia), and
    Lesson 31.39.9: Meticulous peri-operative control of BP.

Chapter 32: Cardio obstetrics
  Mini Chapter 32.1: Epidemiology
    (treated as Lesson itself)
  Mini Chapter 32.2: Physiological adaptations to pregnancy
    Lesson 32.2.1: During the first trimester (from conception to 13 weeks and 6 days of gestation)
    Lesson 32.2.2: 50% increase in renal flow and glomerular filtration rates by the end of the first trimester.
    Lesson 32.2.3: During the third trimester (from 28 weeks and 0 days of gestation through delivery)
    Lesson 32.2.4: Heart rate peaks in the late third trimester, with a 20% to 25% increase relative to baseline.
    Lesson 32.2.5: During active labor, SBP and DBP can increase an additional 15-25% and 10-15% respectively.
    Lesson 32.2.6: Cardiac output is increased by 15% in early labor and 25% during the active phase.
  Mini Chapter 32.3: Pre-pregnancy counselling
    Lesson 32.3.1: Predictors of maternal CV events
    Lesson 32.3.2: Prior cardiac event (heart failure, transient ischaemic attack, stroke, arrhythmia).
    Lesson 32.3.3: NYHA class III/IV.
    Lesson 32.3.4: Left heart obstruction (moderate to severe).
    Lesson 32.3.5: Reduced systemic ventricular systolic function (EF < 40%).
    Lesson 32.3.6: Reduced subpulmonary ventricular function (TAPSE < 16 mm).
    Lesson 32.3.7: Systemic atrioventricular valve regurgitation (moderate to severe).
    Lesson 32.3.8: Pulmonary atrioventricular valve regurgitation (moderate to severe).
    Lesson 32.3.9: Pulmonary arterial hypertension.
    Lesson 32.3.10: Cardiac medication before pregnancy.
    Lesson 32.3.11: Cyanosis (O2 saturation < 90%)
    Lesson 32.3.12: Smoking history
    Lesson 32.3.13: Predictors of neonatal events
    Lesson 32.3.14: NYHA class III/IV or cyanosis during baseline pre-natal visit
    Lesson 32.3.15: Maternal left heart obstruction
    Lesson 32.3.16: Smoking during pregnancy

  Mini Chapter 32.38: Cardiovascular drugs and pregnancy
    Lesson 32.38.1: ACE‐I, ARB, aldosterone antagonists: contraindicated during pregnancy.
    Lesson 32.38.2: Statins, ezetimibe: contraindicated.
    Lesson 32.38.3: Aspirin is safe.
    Lesson 32.38.4: Clopidogrel: probably safe, but limited data.
    Lesson 32.38.5: Adenosine: safe
    Lesson 32.38.6: Digoxin: safe.
    Lesson 32.38.7: Diuretics: may be used to treat HF. Avoid the aggressive use, as they may reduce placental flow.
    Lesson 32.38.8: Amiodarone: crosses the placenta and may cause neonatal goiter. May be used if necessary.
    Lesson 32.38.9: Sotalol, flecainide: relatively safe.
    Lesson 32.38.10: Hydralazine, methyldopa: safe.
    Lesson 32.38.11: Nitrates: may be used in HF, but preferably avoided.
